<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Blood</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Organic fluid which transports nutrients throughout the organism</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Blood_(disambiguation)" title="Blood (disambiguation)">Blood (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terminology" title="Anatomical terminology">Anatomical terminology</a><div style="text-align: right;"><small class="noprint">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q7873" title="d:Q7873">edit on Wikidata</a>]</small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Blood</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Body_fluid" title="Body fluid">body fluid</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> that delivers necessary substances such as <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, and transports <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste" title="Metabolic waste">metabolic waste products</a> away from those same cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Blood in the circulatory system is also known as <i>peripheral blood</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Blood_cell" title="Blood cell">blood cells</a> it carries, <i>peripheral blood cells</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancer_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-cancer-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Blood is composed of blood cells suspended in <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">blood plasma</a>. Plasma, which constitutes 55% of blood fluid, is mostly water (92% by volume),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and contains <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, mineral <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (plasma being the main medium for excretory product transportation), and blood cells themselves. <a href="/wiki/Albumin" title="Albumin">Albumin</a> is the main protein in plasma, and it functions to regulate the <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloidal</a> <a href="/wiki/Osmotic_pressure" title="Osmotic pressure">osmotic pressure</a> of blood.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The blood cells are mainly <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a> (also called RBCs or erythrocytes), <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a> (also called WBCs or leukocytes) and <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a> (also called thrombocytes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The most abundant cells in vertebrate blood are red blood cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> These contain <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>, an iron-containing protein, which facilitates oxygen transport by reversibly binding to this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breath" title="Breath">respiratory</a> gas thereby increasing its solubility in blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In contrast, carbon dioxide is mostly transported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extracellular" title="Extracellular">extracellularly</a> as <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> ion transported in plasma.
</p><p>Vertebrate blood is bright red when its hemoglobin is oxygenated and dark red when it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deoxygenated_hemoglobin" title="Deoxygenated hemoglobin">deoxygenated</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a>, use <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a> to carry oxygen, instead of hemoglobin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Insects and some mollusks use a fluid called <a href="/wiki/Hemolymph" title="Hemolymph">hemolymph</a> instead of blood, the difference being that hemolymph is not contained in a closed <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a>. In most insects, this "blood" does not contain oxygen-carrying molecules such as hemoglobin because their bodies are small enough for their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invertebrate_trachea" title="Invertebrate trachea">tracheal system</a> to suffice for supplying oxygen.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gnathostomata" title="Gnathostomata">Jawed vertebrates</a> have an <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_immune_system" title="Adaptive immune system">adaptive immune system</a>, based largely on white blood cells. White blood cells help to resist infections and parasites. Platelets are important in the <a href="/wiki/Coagulation" title="Coagulation">clotting</a> of blood. <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">Arthropods</a>, using hemolymph, have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemocyte" title="Hemocyte">hemocytes</a> as part of their <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>.
</p><p>Blood is circulated around the body through <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> by the pumping action of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>. In animals with <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arterial</a> blood carries oxygen from inhaled air to the tissues of the body, and <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">venous</a> blood carries carbon dioxide, a waste product of <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> produced by cells, from the tissues to the lungs to be exhaled.
</p><p>Medical terms related to blood often begin with <b>hemo-</b> or <b>hemato-</b> (<a href="/wiki/American_and_British_English_spelling_differences#ae_and_oe" title="American and British English spelling differences">also spelled</a> <b>haemo-</b> and <b>haemato-</b>) from the Greek word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">αἷμα</span></span> (<i>haima</i>) for "blood". In terms of <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histology</a>, blood is considered a specialized form of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krause2005_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krause2005-9">[9]</a></sup> given its origin in the bones and the presence of potential molecular fibers in the form of <a href="/wiki/Fibrinogen" title="Fibrinogen">fibrinogen</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions">Functions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1GZX_Haemoglobin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/220px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/330px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/440px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1GZX_Haemoglobin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hemoglobin, a globular protein<br/>green = haem (or heme) groups<br/>red &amp; blue = protein subunits</div></div></div>
<p>Blood performs many important functions within the body, including:
</p>
<ul><li>Supply of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to tissues (bound to <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>, which is carried in red cells)</li>
<li>Supply of nutrients such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> (dissolved in the blood or bound to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_proteins" title="Blood proteins">plasma proteins</a> (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_lipid" title="Blood lipid">blood lipids</a>))</li>
<li>Removal of waste such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a></li>
<li>Immunological functions, including circulation of <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a>, and detection of foreign material by <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coagulation" title="Coagulation">Coagulation</a>, the response to a broken blood vessel, the conversion of blood from a liquid to a semisolid gel to stop bleeding</li>
<li>Messenger functions, including the transport of <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> and the signaling of <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a> damage</li>
<li>Regulation of core <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_temperature" title="Body temperature">body temperature</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hydraulics" title="Hydraulics">Hydraulic</a> functions</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constituents">Constituents</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_mammals">In mammals</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reference_ranges_for_common_blood_tests" title="Reference ranges for common blood tests">Reference ranges for common blood tests</a></div>
<p>Blood accounts for 7% of the human body weight,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alberts_table_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-alberts_table-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysFactVol_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhysFactVol-11">[11]</a></sup> with an average density around 1060 kg/m<sup>3</sup>, very close to pure water's density of 1000 kg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The average adult has a <a href="/wiki/Blood_volume" title="Blood volume">blood volume</a> of roughly 5 <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litres</a> (11 US pt) or 1.3 gallons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysFactVol_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-PhysFactVol-11">[11]</a></sup> which is composed of plasma and <i>formed elements</i>. The formed elements are the two types of blood cell or <i>corpuscle</i> – the <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, (erythrocytes) and <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cells</a> (leukocytes), and the cell fragments called <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEER_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEER-13">[13]</a></sup> that are involved in clotting. By volume, the red blood cells constitute about 45% of whole blood, the plasma about 54.3%, and white cells about 0.7%.
</p><p>Whole blood (plasma and cells) exhibits <a href="/wiki/Non-Newtonian_fluid" title="Non-Newtonian fluid">non-Newtonian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_viscosity" title="Blood viscosity">fluid dynamics</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title='"Non-Newtonian" encompasses several different types of properties. (April 2021)'>specify</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Krew_Frakcjonowana.jpg" title="Human blood fractioned by centrifugation: Plasma (upper, yellow layer), buffy coat (middle, thin white layer) and erythrocyte layer (bottom, red layer) can be seen."><img alt="Human blood fractioned by centrifugation: Plasma (upper, yellow layer), buffy coat (middle, thin white layer) and erythrocyte layer (bottom, red layer) can be seen." data-file-height="1583" data-file-width="890" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Krew_Frakcjonowana.jpg/67px-Krew_Frakcjonowana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Krew_Frakcjonowana.jpg/101px-Krew_Frakcjonowana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Krew_Frakcjonowana.jpg/135px-Krew_Frakcjonowana.jpg 2x" width="67"/></a></div></div>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cells">Cells</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Complete_blood_count" title="Complete blood count">Complete blood count</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_White_Blood_cells.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Red_White_Blood_cells.jpg/220px-Red_White_Blood_cells.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Red_White_Blood_cells.jpg/330px-Red_White_Blood_cells.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Red_White_Blood_cells.jpg/440px-Red_White_Blood_cells.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_White_Blood_cells.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">scanning electron microscope</a> (SEM) image of a normal <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cell</a> (left), a <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelet</a> (middle), and a <a href="/wiki/White_blood_cell" title="White blood cell">white blood cell</a> (right)</div></div></div>
<p>One microliter of blood contains:
</p>
<ul><li><b>4.7 to 6.1 million (male), 4.2 to 5.4 million (female) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erythrocyte" title="Erythrocyte">erythrocytes</a>:</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Red blood cells contain the blood's <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> and distribute oxygen. Mature red blood cells lack a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a> in mammals. The red blood cells (together with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothelial" title="Endothelial">endothelial</a> vessel cells and other cells) are also marked by <a href="/wiki/Glycoprotein" title="Glycoprotein">glycoproteins</a> that define the different <a href="/wiki/Human_blood_group_systems" title="Human blood group systems">blood types</a>. The proportion of blood occupied by red blood cells is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Hematocrit" title="Hematocrit">hematocrit</a>, and is normally about 45%. The combined surface area of all red blood cells of the human body would be roughly 2,000 times as great as the body's exterior surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>4,000–11,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leukocyte" title="Leukocyte">leukocytes</a>:</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong_WF_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong_WF-16">[16]</a></sup> White blood cells are part of the body's <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>; they destroy and remove old or aberrant cells and cellular debris, as well as attack infectious agents (<a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>) and foreign substances. The cancer of leukocytes is called <a href="/wiki/Leukemia" title="Leukemia">leukemia</a>.</li>
<li><b>200,000–500,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thrombocytes" title="Thrombocytes">thrombocytes</a>:</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganong_WF_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ganong_WF-16">[16]</a></sup> Also called <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelets</a>, they take part in blood clotting (<a href="/wiki/Coagulation" title="Coagulation">coagulation</a>). Fibrin from the coagulation cascade creates a mesh over the <a href="/wiki/Platelet_plug" title="Platelet plug">platelet plug</a>.</li></ul>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:15px">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma">Plasma</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">Blood plasma</a></div>
<p>About 55% of blood is <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">blood plasma</a>, a fluid that is the blood's liquid medium, which by itself is straw-yellow in color. The blood plasma volume totals of 2.7–3.0 liters (2.8–3.2 quarts) in an average human. It is essentially an <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">aqueous</a> solution containing 92% water, 8% blood plasma <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, and trace amounts of other materials. Plasma circulates dissolved nutrients, such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> (dissolved in the blood or bound to plasma proteins), and removes waste products, such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a>.
</p><p>Other important <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_blood_components" title="List of human blood components">components</a> include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Serum_albumin" title="Serum albumin">Serum albumin</a></li>
<li>Blood-clotting factors (to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Coagulation" title="Coagulation">coagulation</a>)</li>
<li>Immunoglobulins (<a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lipoprotein" title="Lipoprotein">lipoprotein</a> particles</li>
<li>Various other <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a></li>
<li>Various <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Sodium_in_biology" title="Sodium in biology">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a>)</li></ul>
<p>The term <b>serum</b> refers to plasma from which the clotting proteins have been removed. Most of the proteins remaining are albumin and <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">immunoglobulins</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="pH_values">pH values</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_homeostasis" title="Acid–base homeostasis">Acid–base homeostasis</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/PH#Living_systems" title="PH">Blood pH</a> is regulated to stay within the narrow range of 7.35 to 7.45, making it slightly <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a> (compensation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waugh_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-19">[19]</a></sup> Extra-cellular fluid in blood that has a pH below 7.35 is too <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acidic</a>, whereas blood pH above 7.45 is too basic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-17">[17]</a></sup> A pH below 6.9 or above 7.8 is usually lethal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-17">[17]</a></sup> Blood pH, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen#partial_pressure" title="Oxygen">partial pressure of oxygen (pO<sub>2</sub>)</a>, <a href="/wiki/PCO2" title="PCO2">partial pressure of carbon dioxide (pCO<sub>2</sub>)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> (HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>) are carefully regulated by a number of <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostatic mechanisms</a>, which exert their influence principally through the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Urinary_system" title="Urinary system">urinary system</a> to control the <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_homeostasis" title="Acid–base homeostasis">acid–base balance</a> and respiration, which is called compensation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-17">[17]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Arterial_blood_gas_test" title="Arterial blood gas test">arterial blood gas test</a> measures these. Plasma also circulates <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> transmitting their messages to various tissues. The list of normal <a href="/wiki/Reference_ranges_for_blood_tests" title="Reference ranges for blood tests">reference ranges</a> for various blood electrolytes is extensive.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_non-mammalian_vertebrates">In non-mammalian vertebrates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Newbloodm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="376" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Newbloodm.jpg/220px-Newbloodm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Newbloodm.jpg/330px-Newbloodm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Newbloodm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Newbloodm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vertebrate red blood cell types, measurements in micrometers</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frogrbc1000x.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="952" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Frogrbc1000x.jpg/220px-Frogrbc1000x.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Frogrbc1000x.jpg/330px-Frogrbc1000x.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Frogrbc1000x.jpg/440px-Frogrbc1000x.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frogrbc1000x.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frog red blood cells magnified 1000 times</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turtlerbc1000x.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="952" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Turtlerbc1000x.jpg/220px-Turtlerbc1000x.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Turtlerbc1000x.jpg/330px-Turtlerbc1000x.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Turtlerbc1000x.jpg/440px-Turtlerbc1000x.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turtlerbc1000x.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Turtle red blood cells magnified 1000 times</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chickenrbc1000x.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="952" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Chickenrbc1000x.jpg/220px-Chickenrbc1000x.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Chickenrbc1000x.jpg/330px-Chickenrbc1000x.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Chickenrbc1000x.jpg/440px-Chickenrbc1000x.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chickenrbc1000x.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chicken red blood cells magnified 1000 times</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Humanrbc1000x.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="952" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Humanrbc1000x.jpg/220px-Humanrbc1000x.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Humanrbc1000x.jpg/330px-Humanrbc1000x.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Humanrbc1000x.jpg/440px-Humanrbc1000x.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Humanrbc1000x.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human red blood cells magnified 1000 times</div></div></div>
<p>Human blood is typical of that of mammals, although the precise details concerning cell numbers, size, <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure" title="Protein structure">protein structure</a>, and so on, vary somewhat between species. In non-mammalian vertebrates, however, there are some key differences:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VB_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-VB-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Red blood cells of non-mammalian vertebrates are flattened and ovoid in form, and retain their cell nuclei.</li>
<li>There is considerable variation in the types and proportions of white blood cells; for example, acidophils are generally more common than in humans.</li>
<li>Platelets are unique to mammals; in other vertebrates, small nucleated, spindle cells called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thrombocytes" title="Thrombocytes">thrombocytes</a> are responsible for blood clotting instead.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulatory_system">Circulatory system</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_the_human_heart_(cropped).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="663" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/220px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/330px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg/440px-Diagram_of_the_human_heart_%28cropped%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_of_the_human_heart_(cropped).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Circulation of blood through the human heart</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">Circulatory system</a></div>
<p>Blood is circulated around the body through <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> by the pumping action of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>. In humans, blood is pumped from the strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_ventricle" title="Left ventricle">left ventricle</a> of the heart through <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a> to peripheral <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a> and returns to the right <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">atrium of the heart</a> through <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a>. It then enters the right <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricle</a> and is pumped through the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a> and returns to the left atrium through the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_vein" title="Pulmonary vein">pulmonary veins</a>. Blood then enters the left ventricle to be circulated again. Arterial blood carries oxygen from inhaled air to all of the cells of the body, and <a href="/wiki/Venous_blood" title="Venous blood">venous blood</a> carries carbon dioxide, a waste product of <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, to the lungs to be exhaled. However, one exception includes pulmonary arteries, which contain the most deoxygenated blood in the body, while the pulmonary veins contain oxygenated blood.
</p><p>Additional return flow may be generated by the movement of <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscles</a>, which can compress veins and push blood through the valves in veins toward the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_atrium" title="Right atrium">right atrium</a>.
</p><p>The blood circulation was famously described by <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a> in 1628.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell_production_and_degradation">Cell production and degradation</span></h3>
<p>In vertebrates, the various cells of blood are made in the <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a> in a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hematopoiesis" title="Hematopoiesis">hematopoiesis</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Erythropoiesis" title="Erythropoiesis">erythropoiesis</a>, the production of red blood cells; and <a href="/wiki/Myelopoiesis" title="Myelopoiesis">myelopoiesis</a>, the production of white blood cells and platelets. During childhood, almost every human bone produces red blood cells; as adults, red blood cell production is limited to the larger bones: the bodies of the vertebrae, the breastbone (sternum), the ribcage, the pelvic bones, and the bones of the upper arms and legs. In addition, during childhood, the <a href="/wiki/Thymus" title="Thymus">thymus</a> gland, found in the <a href="/wiki/Mediastinum" title="Mediastinum">mediastinum</a>, is an important source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_lymphocytes" title="T lymphocytes">T lymphocytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
The proteinaceous component of blood (including clotting proteins) is produced predominantly by the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, while hormones are produced by the <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_gland" title="Endocrine gland">endocrine glands</a> and the watery fraction is regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">hypothalamus</a> and maintained by the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidney</a>.
</p><p>Healthy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erythrocytes" title="Erythrocytes">erythrocytes</a> have a plasma life of about 120 days before they are degraded by the <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kupffer_cell" title="Kupffer cell">Kupffer cells</a> in the liver. The liver also clears some proteins, lipids, and amino acids. The kidney actively secretes waste products into the <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxygen_transport">Oxygen transport</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_saturation_(medicine)" title="Oxygen saturation (medicine)">Oxygen saturation (medicine)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxyhaemoglobin_dissociation_curve.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="439" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oxyhaemoglobin_dissociation_curve.png/220px-Oxyhaemoglobin_dissociation_curve.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oxyhaemoglobin_dissociation_curve.png/330px-Oxyhaemoglobin_dissociation_curve.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Oxyhaemoglobin_dissociation_curve.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxyhaemoglobin_dissociation_curve.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Basic hemoglobin saturation curve. It is moved to the right in higher acidity (more dissolved carbon dioxide) and to the left in lower acidity (less dissolved carbon dioxide)</div></div></div>
<p>About 98.5%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> in a sample of arterial blood in a healthy human breathing air at sea-level pressure is chemically combined with the <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>. About 1.5% is physically dissolved in the other blood liquids and not connected to hemoglobin. The hemoglobin molecule is the primary transporter of oxygen in <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> and many other species (for exceptions, see below). Hemoglobin has an oxygen binding capacity between 1.36 and 1.40 ml O<sub>2</sub> per gram hemoglobin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> which increases the total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_oxygen_capacity" title="Blood oxygen capacity">blood oxygen capacity</a> seventyfold,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brsphys_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-brsphys-25">[25]</a></sup> compared to if oxygen solely were carried by its solubility of 0.03 ml O<sub>2</sub> per liter blood per mm Hg partial pressure of oxygen (about 100 mm Hg in arteries).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brsphys_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-brsphys-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the exception of pulmonary and <a href="/wiki/Umbilical_artery" title="Umbilical artery">umbilical arteries</a> and their corresponding veins, arteries carry <b>oxygenated blood</b> away from the heart and deliver it to the body via <a href="/wiki/Arteriole" title="Arteriole">arterioles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillaries</a>, where the oxygen is consumed; afterwards, <a href="/wiki/Venule" title="Venule">venules</a> and veins carry <b>deoxygenated blood</b> back to the heart.
</p><p>Under normal conditions in adult humans at rest, hemoglobin in blood leaving the lungs is about 98–99% <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_saturation_(medicine)" title="Oxygen saturation (medicine)">saturated with oxygen</a>, achieving an oxygen delivery between 950 and 1150 ml/min<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edwards_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-edwards-26">[26]</a></sup> to the body. In a healthy adult at rest, oxygen consumption is approximately 200–250 ml/min,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edwards_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-edwards-26">[26]</a></sup> and deoxygenated blood returning to the lungs is still roughly 75%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> (70 to 78%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edwards_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-edwards-26">[26]</a></sup> saturated. Increased oxygen consumption during sustained exercise reduces the oxygen saturation of venous blood, which can reach less than 15% in a trained athlete; although breathing rate and blood flow increase to compensate, oxygen saturation in arterial blood can drop to 95% or less under these conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Oxygen saturation this low is considered dangerous in an individual at rest (for instance, during surgery under anesthesia). Sustained hypoxia (oxygenation less than 90%), is dangerous to health, and severe hypoxia (saturations less than 30%) may be rapidly fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a>, receiving oxygen via the <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a>, is exposed to much lower oxygen pressures (about 21% of the level found in an adult's lungs), so fetuses produce another form of hemoglobin with a much higher affinity for oxygen (<a href="/wiki/Fetal_hemoglobin" title="Fetal hemoglobin">hemoglobin F</a>) to function under these conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide_transport">Carbon dioxide transport</span></h3>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> is carried in blood in three different ways. (The exact percentages vary depending whether it is arterial or venous blood). Most of it (about 70%) is converted to bicarbonate ions <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a> in the red blood cells by the reaction CO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O → H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> → H<sup>+</sup> + <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>; about 7% is dissolved in the plasma; and about 23% is bound to hemoglobin as <a href="/wiki/Carbamino" title="Carbamino">carbamino</a> compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hemoglobin, the main oxygen-carrying molecule in red blood cells, carries both oxygen and carbon dioxide. However, the CO<sub>2</sub> bound to hemoglobin does not bind to the same site as oxygen. Instead, it combines with the N-terminal groups on the four globin chains. However, because of <a href="/wiki/Allosteric_regulation" title="Allosteric regulation">allosteric</a> effects on the hemoglobin molecule, the binding of CO<sub>2</sub> decreases the amount of oxygen that is bound for a given partial pressure of oxygen. The decreased binding to carbon dioxide in the blood due to increased oxygen levels is known as the <a href="/wiki/Haldane_effect" title="Haldane effect">Haldane effect</a>, and is important in the transport of carbon dioxide from the tissues to the lungs. A rise in the partial pressure of CO<sub>2</sub> or a lower pH will cause offloading of oxygen from hemoglobin, which is known as the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_effect" title="Bohr effect">Bohr effect</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_of_hydrogen_ions">Transport of hydrogen ions</span></h3>
<p>Some oxyhemoglobin loses oxygen and becomes deoxyhemoglobin. Deoxyhemoglobin binds most of the hydrogen ions as it has a much greater affinity for more hydrogen than does oxyhemoglobin.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lymphatic_system">Lymphatic system</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lymphatic_system" title="Lymphatic system">Lymphatic system</a></div>
<p>In mammals, blood is in equilibrium with <a href="/wiki/Lymph" title="Lymph">lymph</a>, which is continuously formed in tissues from blood by capillary ultrafiltration. Lymph is collected by a system of small lymphatic vessels and directed to the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_duct" title="Thoracic duct">thoracic duct</a>, which drains into the left <a href="/wiki/Subclavian_vein" title="Subclavian vein">subclavian vein</a>, where lymph rejoins the systemic blood circulation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermoregulation">Thermoregulation</span></h3>
<p>Blood circulation transports heat throughout the body, and adjustments to this flow are an important part of <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulation</a>. Increasing blood flow to the surface (e.g., during warm weather or strenuous exercise) causes warmer skin, resulting in faster heat loss. In contrast, when the external temperature is low, blood flow to the extremities and surface of the skin is reduced and to prevent heat loss and is circulated to the important organs of the body, preferentially.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rate_of_flow">Rate of flow</span></h3>
<p>Rate of blood flow varies greatly between different organs. Liver has the most abundant blood supply with an approximate flow of 1350 ml/min. Kidney and brain are the second and the third most supplied organs, with 1100 ml/min and ~700 ml/min, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Relative rates of blood flow per 100 g of tissue are different, with kidney, adrenal gland and thyroid being the first, second and third most supplied tissues, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydraulic_functions">Hydraulic functions</span></h3>
<p>The restriction of blood flow can also be used in specialized tissues to cause engorgement, resulting in an <a href="/wiki/Erection" title="Erection">erection</a> of that tissue; examples are the <a href="/wiki/Erectile_tissue" title="Erectile tissue">erectile tissue</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Penis" title="Penis">penis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clitoris" title="Clitoris">clitoris</a>.
</p><p>Another example of a hydraulic function is the <a href="/wiki/Jumping_spider" title="Jumping spider">jumping spider</a>, in which blood forced into the legs under pressure causes them to straighten for a powerful jump, without the need for bulky muscular legs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span></h3>
<p>In insects, the blood (more properly called <a href="/wiki/Hemolymph" title="Hemolymph">hemolymph</a>) is not involved in the transport of oxygen. (Openings called <a href="/wiki/Trachea#Invertebrates" title="Trachea">tracheae</a> allow oxygen from the air to diffuse directly to the tissues.) Insect blood moves nutrients to the tissues and removes waste products in an open system.
</p><p>Other invertebrates use respiratory proteins to increase the oxygen-carrying capacity. Hemoglobin is the most common respiratory protein found in nature. <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">Hemocyanin</a> (blue) contains copper and is found in <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">mollusks</a>. It is thought that <a href="/wiki/Tunicate" title="Tunicate">tunicates</a> (sea squirts) might use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanabins" title="Vanabins">vanabins</a> (proteins containing <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>) for <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_pigment" title="Respiratory pigment">respiratory pigment</a> (bright-green, blue, or orange).
</p><p>In many invertebrates, these oxygen-carrying proteins are freely soluble in the blood; in vertebrates they are contained in specialized red blood cells, allowing for a higher concentration of respiratory pigments without increasing <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> or damaging blood filtering organs like the kidneys.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_tube_worm" title="Giant tube worm">Giant tube worms</a> have unusual hemoglobins that allow them to live in extraordinary environments. These hemoglobins also carry sulfides normally fatal in other animals.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Color">Color</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
<p>The coloring matter of blood (<b>hemochrome</b>) is largely due to the protein in the blood responsible for oxygen transport. Different groups of organisms use different proteins.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hemoglobin">Hemoglobin</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">Hemoglobin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bleeding_finger.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bleeding_finger.jpg/220px-Bleeding_finger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bleeding_finger.jpg/330px-Bleeding_finger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bleeding_finger.jpg/440px-Bleeding_finger.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bleeding_finger.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Capillary blood from a bleeding finger</div></div></div>
<p>Hemoglobin is the principal determinant of the color of blood in vertebrates. Each molecule has four heme groups, and their interaction with various molecules alters the exact color. In <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> and other hemoglobin-using creatures, arterial blood and capillary blood are bright red, as oxygen imparts a strong red color to the heme group. Deoxygenated blood is a darker shade of red; this is present in veins, and can be seen during <a href="/wiki/Blood_donation" title="Blood donation">blood donation</a> and when venous blood samples are taken. This is because the spectrum of light absorbed by hemoglobin differs between the oxygenated and deoxygenated states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Blood in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">carbon monoxide poisoning</a> is bright red, because <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> causes the formation of <a href="/wiki/Carboxyhemoglobin" title="Carboxyhemoglobin">carboxyhemoglobin</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a> poisoning, the body cannot utilize oxygen, so the venous blood remains oxygenated, increasing the redness. There are some conditions affecting the heme groups present in hemoglobin that can make the skin appear blue – a symptom called <a href="/wiki/Cyanosis" title="Cyanosis">cyanosis</a>. If the heme is oxidized, <a href="/wiki/Methemoglobin" title="Methemoglobin">methemoglobin</a>, which is more brownish and cannot transport oxygen, is formed. In the rare condition <a href="/wiki/Sulfhemoglobinemia" title="Sulfhemoglobinemia">sulfhemoglobinemia</a>, arterial hemoglobin is partially oxygenated, and appears dark red with a bluish hue.
</p><p>Veins close to the surface of the skin appear blue for a variety of reasons. However, the factors that contribute to this alteration of <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">color perception</a> are related to the light-scattering properties of the skin and the processing of visual input by the <a href="/wiki/Visual_cortex" title="Visual cortex">visual cortex</a>, rather than the actual color of the venous blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Skink" title="Skink">Skinks</a> in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Prasinohaema" title="Prasinohaema">Prasinohaema</a></i> have green blood due to a buildup of the waste product <a href="/wiki/Biliverdin" title="Biliverdin">biliverdin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hemocyanin">Hemocyanin</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">Hemocyanin</a></div>
<p>The blood of most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusks" title="Mollusks">mollusks</a> – including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cephalopods" title="Cephalopods">cephalopods</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropods" title="Gastropods">gastropods</a> – as well as some <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_crab" title="Horseshoe crab">horseshoe crabs</a>, is blue, as it contains the copper-containing protein hemocyanin at concentrations of about 50 grams per liter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHC_2004_p276-7_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-AHC_2004_p276-7-39">[39]</a></sup> Hemocyanin is colorless when deoxygenated and dark blue when oxygenated. The blood in the circulation of these creatures, which generally live in cold environments with low oxygen tensions, is grey-white to pale yellow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHC_2004_p276-7_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-AHC_2004_p276-7-39">[39]</a></sup> and it turns dark blue when exposed to the oxygen in the air, as seen when they bleed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHC_2004_p276-7_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-AHC_2004_p276-7-39">[39]</a></sup> This is due to change in color of hemocyanin when it is oxidized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHC_2004_p276-7_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-AHC_2004_p276-7-39">[39]</a></sup> Hemocyanin carries oxygen in <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_fluid" title="Extracellular fluid">extracellular fluid</a>, which is in contrast to the intracellular oxygen transport in mammals by hemoglobin in RBCs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHC_2004_p276-7_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-AHC_2004_p276-7-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chlorocruorin">Chlorocruorin</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorocruorin" title="Chlorocruorin">Chlorocruorin</a></div>
<p>The blood of most <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">annelid</a> worms and some marine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polychaetes" title="Polychaetes">polychaetes</a> use chlorocruorin to transport oxygen. It is green in color in dilute solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceAnswer_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceAnswer-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hemerythrin">Hemerythrin</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hemerythrin" title="Hemerythrin">Hemerythrin</a></div>
<p>Hemerythrin is used for oxygen transport in the marine invertebrates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sipunculid" title="Sipunculid">sipunculids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Priapulid" title="Priapulid">priapulids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">brachiopods</a>, and the annelid worm, magelona. Hemerythrin is violet-pink when oxygenated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceAnswer_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceAnswer-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hemovanadin">Hemovanadin</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hemovanadin" title="Hemovanadin">Hemovanadin</a></div>
<p>The blood of some species of <a href="/wiki/Ascidiacea" title="Ascidiacea">ascidians</a> and tunicates, also known as sea squirts, contains proteins called vanadins. These proteins are based on <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>, and give the creatures a concentration of vanadium in their bodies 100 times higher than the surrounding seawater. Unlike hemocyanin and hemoglobin, hemovanadin is not an oxygen carrier. When exposed to oxygen, however, vanadins turn a mustard yellow.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disorders">Disorders</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_medical">General medical</span></h3>
<ul><li>Disorders of volume
<ul><li>Injury can cause blood loss through bleeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> A healthy adult can lose almost 20% of blood volume (1 L) before the first symptom, restlessness, begins, and 40% of volume (2 L) before <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a> sets in. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thrombocyte" title="Thrombocyte">Thrombocytes</a> are important for blood <a href="/wiki/Coagulation" title="Coagulation">coagulation</a> and the formation of blood clots, which can stop bleeding. Trauma to the internal organs or bones can cause <a href="/wiki/Internal_bleeding" title="Internal bleeding">internal bleeding</a>, which can sometimes be severe.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">Dehydration</a> can reduce the blood volume by reducing the water content of the blood. This would rarely result in <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a> (apart from the very severe cases) but may result in <a href="/wiki/Orthostatic_hypotension" title="Orthostatic hypotension">orthostatic hypotension</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fainting" title="Fainting">fainting</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li>Disorders of circulation
<ul><li>Shock is the ineffective <a href="/wiki/Perfusion" title="Perfusion">perfusion</a> of tissues, and can be caused by a variety of conditions including blood loss, infection, poor <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_output" title="Cardiac output">cardiac output</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">Atherosclerosis</a> reduces the flow of blood through arteries, because atheroma lines arteries and narrows them. Atheroma tends to increase with age, and its progression can be compounded by many causes including smoking, <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">high blood pressure</a>, excess circulating lipids (<a href="/wiki/Hyperlipidemia" title="Hyperlipidemia">hyperlipidemia</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a> mellitus.</li>
<li>Coagulation can form a <a href="/wiki/Thrombosis" title="Thrombosis">thrombosis</a>, which can obstruct vessels.</li>
<li>Problems with blood composition, the pumping action of the heart, or narrowing of blood vessels can have many consequences including hypoxia (lack of oxygen) of the tissues supplied. The term <i>ischemia</i> refers to tissue that is inadequately perfused with blood, and <i>infarction</i> refers to tissue death (<a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a>), which can occur when the blood supply has been blocked (or is very inadequate).</li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hematological">Hematological</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hematology" title="Hematology">Hematology</a></div>
<ul><li>Anemia
<ul><li>Insufficient red cell mass (<a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>) can be the result of bleeding, blood disorders like <a href="/wiki/Thalassemia" title="Thalassemia">thalassemia</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illnesses_related_to_poor_nutrition" title="Illnesses related to poor nutrition">nutritional deficiencies</a>, and may require one or more <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">blood transfusions</a>. Anemia can also be due to a <a href="/wiki/Genetic_disorder" title="Genetic disorder">genetic disorder</a> in which the red blood cells simply do not function effectively. Anemia can be confirmed by a <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood test</a> if the hemoglobin value is less than 13.5 gm/dl in men or less than 12.0 gm/dl in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Several countries have <a href="/wiki/Blood_bank" title="Blood bank">blood banks</a> to fill the demand for transfusable blood. A person receiving a blood transfusion must have a <a href="/wiki/Blood_type" title="Blood type">blood type</a> compatible with that of the donor.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_anemia" title="Sickle-cell anemia">Sickle-cell anemia</a></li></ul></li>
<li>Disorders of cell proliferation
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Leukemia" title="Leukemia">Leukemia</a> is a group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cancer_(medicine)" title="Cancer (medicine)">cancers</a> of the blood-forming tissues and cells.</li>
<li>Non-cancerous overproduction of red cells (<a href="/wiki/Polycythemia_vera" title="Polycythemia vera">polycythemia vera</a>) or platelets (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_thrombocytosis" title="Essential thrombocytosis">essential thrombocytosis</a>) may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premalignant_condition" title="Premalignant condition">premalignant</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Myelodysplastic_syndrome" title="Myelodysplastic syndrome">Myelodysplastic syndromes</a> involve ineffective production of one or more cell lines.</li></ul></li>
<li>Disorders of coagulation
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemophilia" title="Hemophilia">Hemophilia</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Genetic_disorder" title="Genetic disorder">genetic illness</a> that causes dysfunction in one of the blood's <a href="/wiki/Coagulation" title="Coagulation">clotting mechanisms</a>. This can allow otherwise inconsequential wounds to be life-threatening, but more commonly results in <a href="/wiki/Hemarthrosis" title="Hemarthrosis">hemarthrosis</a>, or bleeding into joint spaces, which can be crippling.</li>
<li>Ineffective or insufficient platelets can also result in <a href="/wiki/Coagulopathy" title="Coagulopathy">coagulopathy</a> (bleeding disorders).</li>
<li>Hypercoagulable state (<a href="/wiki/Thrombophilia" title="Thrombophilia">thrombophilia</a>) results from defects in regulation of platelet or clotting factor function, and can cause thrombosis.</li></ul></li>
<li>Infectious disorders of blood
<ul><li>Blood is an important vector of infection. HIV, the virus that causes AIDS, is transmitted through contact with blood, semen or other body secretions of an infected person. <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">Hepatitis B</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_C" title="Hepatitis C">C</a> are transmitted primarily through blood contact. Owing to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood-borne_infection" title="Blood-borne infection">blood-borne infections</a>, bloodstained objects are treated as a <a href="/wiki/Biological_hazard" title="Biological hazard">biohazard</a>.</li>
<li>Bacterial infection of the blood is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacteremia" title="Bacteremia">bacteremia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>. Viral Infection is viremia. <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">Malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trypanosomiasis" title="Trypanosomiasis">trypanosomiasis</a> are blood-borne parasitic infections.</li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_monoxide_poisoning">Carbon monoxide poisoning</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">Carbon monoxide poisoning</a></div>
<p>Substances other than oxygen can bind to hemoglobin; in some cases, this can cause irreversible damage to the body. Carbon monoxide, for example, is extremely dangerous when carried to the blood via the lungs by inhalation, because carbon monoxide irreversibly binds to hemoglobin to form carboxyhemoglobin, so that less hemoglobin is free to bind oxygen, and fewer oxygen molecules can be transported throughout the blood. This can cause suffocation insidiously. A fire burning in an enclosed room with poor ventilation presents a very dangerous hazard, since it can create a build-up of carbon monoxide in the air. Some carbon monoxide binds to hemoglobin when smoking tobacco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatments">Treatments</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transfusion">Transfusion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">Blood transfusion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bloodbags.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="755" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Bloodbags.jpg/220px-Bloodbags.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Bloodbags.jpg/330px-Bloodbags.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Bloodbags.jpg/440px-Bloodbags.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bloodbags.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Venous blood collected during blood donation</div></div></div>
<p>Blood for transfusion is obtained from human donors by blood donation and stored in a <a href="/wiki/Blood_bank" title="Blood bank">blood bank</a>. There are many different <a href="/wiki/Blood_type" title="Blood type">blood types</a> in humans, the <a href="/wiki/ABO_blood_group_system" title="ABO blood group system">ABO blood group system</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhesus_blood_group_system" title="Rhesus blood group system">Rhesus blood group system</a> being the most important. Transfusion of blood of an incompatible blood group may cause severe, often fatal, complications, so <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crossmatching" title="Crossmatching">crossmatching</a> is done to ensure that a compatible blood product is transfused.
</p><p>Other blood products administered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous" title="Intravenous">intravenously</a> are platelets, blood plasma, cryoprecipitate, and specific coagulation factor concentrates.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intravenous_administration">Intravenous administration</span></h3>
<p>Many forms of medication (from <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> to <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a>) are administered intravenously, as they are not readily or adequately absorbed by the digestive tract.
</p><p>After severe acute blood loss, liquid preparations, generically known as plasma expanders, can be given intravenously, either solutions of salts (NaCl, KCl, CaCl<sub>2</sub> etc.) at physiological concentrations, or colloidal solutions, such as dextrans, <a href="/wiki/Human_serum_albumin" title="Human serum albumin">human serum albumin</a>, or fresh frozen plasma. In these emergency situations, a plasma expander is a more effective life-saving procedure than a blood transfusion, because the metabolism of transfused red blood cells does not restart immediately after a transfusion.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letting">Letting</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bloodletting" title="Bloodletting">bloodletting</a></div>
<p>In modern <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>, bloodletting is used in management of a few rare diseases, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemochromatosis" title="Hemochromatosis">hemochromatosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polycythemia" title="Polycythemia">polycythemia</a>. However, <a href="/wiki/Bloodletting" title="Bloodletting">bloodletting</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hirudotherapy" title="Hirudotherapy">leeching</a> were common unvalidated interventions used until the 19th century, as many diseases were incorrectly thought to be due to an excess of blood, according to <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocratic</a> medicine.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jan_Jansk%C3%BD,_1902.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="258" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Jan_Jansk%C3%BD%2C_1902.jpg/170px-Jan_Jansk%C3%BD%2C_1902.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Jan_Jansk%C3%BD%2C_1902.jpg/255px-Jan_Jansk%C3%BD%2C_1902.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Jan_Jansk%C3%BD%2C_1902.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jan_Jansk%C3%BD,_1902.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jan_Jansk%C3%BD" title="Jan Janský">Jan Janský</a> is credited with the first classification of blood into four types (A, B, AB, and O)</div></div></div>
<p>English <i>blood</i> (<a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>blod</i>) derives from <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> and has cognates with a similar range of meanings in all other Germanic languages (e.g. German <i>Blut</i>, Swedish <i>blod</i>, Gothic <i>blōþ</i>). There is no accepted <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Indo-European</a> etymology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Greek_medicine">Classical Greek medicine</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robin_F%C3%A5hr%C3%A6us" title="Robin Fåhræus">Robin Fåhræus</a> (a Swedish physician who devised the <a href="/wiki/Erythrocyte_sedimentation_rate" title="Erythrocyte sedimentation rate">erythrocyte sedimentation rate</a>) suggested that the Ancient Greek system of <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">humorism</a>, wherein the body was thought to contain four distinct bodily fluids (associated with different temperaments), were based upon the observation of blood clotting in a transparent container. When blood is drawn in a glass container and left undisturbed for about an hour, four different layers can be seen. A dark clot forms at the bottom (the "black bile"). Above the clot is a layer of red blood cells (the "blood"). Above this is a whitish layer of white blood cells (the "phlegm"). The top layer is clear yellow serum (the "yellow bile").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (January 2015)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h3>
<p>The ABO blood group system was discovered in the year 1900 by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Landsteiner" title="Karl Landsteiner">Karl Landsteiner</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jan_Jansk%C3%BD" title="Jan Janský">Jan Janský</a> is credited with the first classification of blood into the four types (A, B, AB, and O) in 1907, which remains in use today. In 1907 the first <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">blood transfusion</a> was performed that used the ABO system to predict compatibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The first non-direct transfusion was performed on 27 March 1914. The Rhesus factor was discovered in 1937.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_religion">Culture and religion</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Blood_libel" title="Blood libel">Blood libel</a></div>
